<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Comparative genomics of two jute species and insight into fibre biogenesis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2017 3:2"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-01-30"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 Macmillan Publishers Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Jute (Corchorus sp.) is one of the most important sources of natural fibre, covering &#8764;80% of global bast fibre production1. Only Corchorus olitorius and Corchorus capsularis are commercially cultivated, though there are more than 100 Corchorus species2 in the Malvaceae family. Here we describe high-quality draft genomes of these two species and their comparisons at the functional genomics level to support tailor-designed breeding. The assemblies cover 91.6% and 82.2% of the estimated genome sizes for C. olitorius and C. capsularis, respectively. In total, 37,031 C. olitorius and 30,096 C. capsularis genes are identified, and most of the genes are validated by cDNA and RNA-seq data. Analyses of clustered gene families and gene collinearity show that jute underwent shared whole-genome duplication &#8764;18.66&#8197;million years (Myr) ago prior to speciation. RNA expression analysis from isolated fibre cells reveals the key regulatory and structural genes involved in fibre formation. This work expands our understanding of the molecular basis of fibre formation laying the foundation for the genetic improvement of jute. High-quality draft genomes have been generated for the two commercially cultivated jute species, Corchorus olitorius and Corchorus capsularis. Transcriptome analyses revealed key regulatory and structural genes involved in fibre formation."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Jute (<i>Corchorus</i> sp.) is one of the most important sources of natural fibre, covering <span class="stix">â¼</span>80% of global bast fibre production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Statistical Bulletin-2014 (Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations, 2014)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d47581e944">1</a></sup>. Only <i>Corchorus olitorius</i> and <i>Corchorus capsularis</i> are commercially cultivated, though there are more than 100 <i>Corchorus</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Saunders, M. Recovery Plan for the Endangered Native Jute Species, Corchorus cunninghamii F. Muell in Queensland (2001â2006) (Natural Heritage Trust, 2006)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d47581e958">2</a></sup> in the Malvaceae family. Here we describe high-quality draft genomes of these two species and their comparisons at the functional genomics level to support tailor-designed breeding. The assemblies cover 91.6% and 82.2% of the estimated genome sizes for <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, respectively. In total, 37,031 <i>C. olitorius</i> and 30,096 <i>C. capsularis</i> genes are identified, and most of the genes are validated by cDNA and RNA-seq data. Analyses of clustered gene families and gene collinearity show that jute underwent shared whole-genome duplication <span class="stix">â¼</span>18.66âmillion years (Myr) ago prior to speciation. RNA expression analysis from isolated fibre cells reveals the key regulatory and structural genes involved in fibre formation. This work expands our understanding of the molecular basis of fibre formation laying the foundation for the genetic improvement of jute.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>
                
                    <div class="c-pdf-button__container">

        </a>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Bast (phloem) fibres are obtained from the stem of the plants such as jute, flax, hemp, ramie and kenaf. The annual global production of jute generates a farm value of <span class="stix">â¼</span>US$2.3âbillion<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Statistical Bulletin-2014 (Food and Agriculture Organization of the United Nations, 2014)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1013">1</a></sup>. The cultivated species of jute, <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, are morphologically and physiologically distinct (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>), and a combination of useful traits from these species into a single genotype is highly desirable<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Mir, J. I. et al. SSR and RAPD profile based grouping of selected jute germplasm with respect to fibre fineness trait. J. Plant Biochem. Biotechnol. 17, 29â35 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1026">3</a></sup>. However, interspecific hybridization is limited because of their cross-incompatibility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Patel, G. I. &amp; Datta, R. M. Interspecific hybridization between Corchorus capsularis L. and C. olitorius L. and the cytological basis of incompatibility between them. Euphytica 9, 89â110 (1960)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1031">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Swaminathan, M. S., Iyer, R. D. &amp; Sulbha, K. Morphology, cytology and breeding behaviour of hybrids between Corchorus olitorius and C. capsularis. Curr. Sci. 30, 67â68 (1961)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1034">5</a></sup>. To facilitate comparative functional genomics and to understand the molecular basis of bast fibre biogenesis, genomes of two popular jute cultivars <i>C. olitorius</i> var. O-4 and <i>C. capsularis</i> var. CVL-1 are sequenced and analysed.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>We performed whole-genome shotgun (WGS) sequencing with the Roche/454 platform (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 1</a>) and assembled the genomes using CABOG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Miller, J. R. et al. Aggressive assembly of pyrosequencing reads with mates. Bioinformatics 24, 2818â2824 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1050">6</a></sup>. The resulting assemblies were 445âMb (scaffold N50 length, 3.3âMb; longest scaffold, 45.5âMb) for <i>C. olitorius</i> and 338âMb (scaffold N50 length, 4.1âMb; longest scaffold, 28.5âMb) for <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 2</a>). Eighty per cent of the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> assemblies were covered with 415 scaffolds (minimum length 76âkb) and 231 scaffolds (minimum length 120âkb), respectively. We estimated the genome sizes for <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> to be <span class="stix">â¼</span>448 and <span class="stix">â¼</span>404âMb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Information and Supplementary Fig. 2</a>), respectively, which were consistent with reported estimates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Benor, S., Fuschs, J. &amp; Blattner, F. R. Genome size variation in Corchorus olitorius (Malvaceae s.l.) and its correlation with elevation and phenotypic traits. Genome 54, 575â585 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1083">7</a></sup>. Whole-genome optical mapping was used to improve the assemblies, resulting increase in N50 of the scaffolds to 4.0âMb for <i>C. olitorius</i> and 8.5âMb for <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Information</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 3â6</a>). We anchored <span class="stix">â¼</span>60% of each assembly to seven genetic linkage groups using a set of 1,389 molecular markers from a consensus genetic linkage map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Joshi, A. et al. Chromosome-specific physical localisation of expressed sequence tag loci in Corchorus olitorius L. Plant Biol. 16, 1133â1139 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1099">8</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Kundu, A. et al. A restriction-site-associated DNA (RAD) linkage map, comparative genomics and identification of QTL for histological fibre content coincident with those for retted bast fibre yield and its major components in jute (Corchorus olitorius L., Malvaceae s.l.). Mol. Breed. 35, 19 (2015)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1102">12</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 7</a>). More than 99% (<i>C. olitorius</i>) and 97% (<i>C. capsularis</i>) of the isotigs generated from transcriptome sequencing of jute seedlings aligned to the respective genomes indicate comprehensive coverage of the gene-rich regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 8 and 9</a>). In addition, more than 97% of the conserved core eukaryotic genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1122">13</a></sup> were present in each of the draft genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 10</a>). Moreover, the single-base accuracy of the <i>de novo</i> assembled genomes was evaluated by mapping all reads onto the scaffolds using a CLC mapper (CLC bio, Aarhus, Denmark). It was observed that 82.29% and 78.28% of the reads are uniquely mapped to <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 11</a>). We predicted 37,031 <i>C. olitorius</i> and 30,096 <i>C. capsularis</i> protein-coding genes by using a combination of <i>de novo</i>, homology and transcriptome-based approaches (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Tab1">Table 1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 12</a>). The averages of gene length, number of exons and coding sequence length of <i>C. olitorius</i> are 2,359âbp, 4.00 and 927âbp respectively and for <i>C. capsularis</i> are 2,759âbp, 4.58 and 1,036âbp respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 13</a>). These values are similar to other malvaceous crops such as cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wang, K. et al. The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. Nat. Genet. 44, 1098â1103 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1171">14</a></sup> and cacao<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1175">15</a></sup>. We also identified 1,010 and 666 microRNAs (miRNAs), 488 and 203 transfer RNAs (tRNAs) and 80 and 110 rRNAs in the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genomes, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Tab1">Table 1</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 14â16</a>). More than 50% of the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genomes were composed of repetitive elements, which is similar to cotton (<span class="stix">â¼</span>57%) and double that of cacao (<span class="stix">â¼</span>24%) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 17 and 18</a>). The proportions of various types of repeats were similar in both genomes, with long terminal repeats being the most abundant (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 19</a>) which is similar to that observed in Bamboo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Peng, Z. et al. The draft genome of the fast-growing non-timber forest species moso bamboo (Phyllostachys heterocycla). Nat. Genet. 45, 456â461 (2013)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1205">16</a></sup> and Banana<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="D'Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213â217 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1209">17</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation features of the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nplants2016223/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>We examined the evolutionary relationship between jute and 13 other sequenced plant genomes with representatives from the Malvids (cacao, cotton and <i>Arabidopsis</i>), Fabids (castor bean, flax, <i>Medicago</i>, soybean, <i>Fragaria</i> and <i>Populus</i>), Asterids (tomato and potato), Vitales (grape) and Monocots (rice). Phylogenetic analysis, based on a concatenated alignment of 13 single-copy gene families from 15 sequenced plant genomes, supported the placement of jute with cacao and cotton in the Malvaceae family (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig1">Fig. 1a</a>). This inclusion is consistent with the analysis done with chloroplast DNA sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Whitlock, B. A., Karol, K. G. &amp; Alverson, W. S. Chloroplast DNA sequences confirm the placement of the enigmatic Oceanopapaver within Corchorus (Grewioideae: Malvaceae s.l., formerly Tiliaceae). Int. J. Plant Sci. 164, 35â41 (2003)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1567">18</a></sup>. All protein-coding genes from 15 genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 20</a>) were clustered into 47,186 gene families (two or more members), of which 8,177 were common to all five groups and 8,816 were confined to the Malvids (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>). Among the Malvids-specific gene families only 613 and 152 are unique to <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig1">Fig. 1b</a>). These jute-specific gene families were significantly enriched with genes related to the oxidationâreduction process, transcription factors, transposases and defence-related proteins (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 21 and 22</a>). To investigate the expression of jute-specific genes in fibre cells, the RNA-seq data from isolated fibre cells and seedlings of <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> were compared. Among the jute-specific genes, Myb/SANT-like domain, Zinc finger, PHD-type, F-box domain cyclin-like proteins are highly expressed in fibre cells than seedlings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 23 and 24</a>) indicating their involvement in bast fibre formation.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Comparative analyses and evolution of the jute genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Comparative analyses and evolution of the jute genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants2016223/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.223/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants2016223_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.223/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants2016223_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="496"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogenetic analysis showing positions of <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> within the Malvids. <b>b</b>, Venn diagram of unique and shared gene families among five Malvids genomes. The analysis was performed with only the Malvids-specific gene families (8,816), as determined in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>. <b>c</b>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions of all paralogous gene pairs in the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genomes. The <i>y</i>-axis shows the percentage of the two-member gene clusters. Myr, million years. <b>d</b>, Syntenic blocks shared between the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> linkage groups (LG).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants2016223/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The natural genetic diversity within the jute species is very narrow<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Palve, S. M. &amp; Sinha, M. K. Genetic variation and interrelationships among fibre yield attributes in secondary gene pool of Corchorus spp. SABRAO J. Breed. Genet. 37, 55â64 (2005)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1663">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Basu, A. et al. Analysis of genetic diversity in cultivated jute determined by means of SSR markers and AFLP profiling. Crop Sci. 44, 678â685 (2004)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1666">20</a></sup> and it is one of the impediments for the breeder to develop high-yield and quality varieties. The extent of gene duplications in the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genomes were examined. By calculating the synonymous substitution rates (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) for paralogous gene pairs, two peaks at 0.24â0.32 and 0.72â0.92 for both of the genomes were found (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). The first peak reveals the whole-genome duplication (WGD) event occurred <span class="stix">â¼</span>18.6 (16.0â21.3) Myr ago prior to their separation at <span class="stix">â¼</span>6âMyr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>). The second peak is indicating an ancient WGD event occurred in jute <span class="stix">â¼</span>129.2 (110.7â141.5) Myr ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig1">Fig. 1c</a>). The ancient duplication event corresponds to the ancient hexaploidization that is shared among the eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiple-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1690">21</a></sup>. Comparison of the two genomes revealed that they share 160 syntenic blocks (five or more genes per block) with the linkage groups covering 58% and 65% of the assembled genome of <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig1">Fig. 1d</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 25 and 26</a>). It indicates that extensive synteny and conserved gene order exists between the genomes. A one-to-one relationship of the predominantly aligned syntenic regions denotes no WGD after speciation (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). The occurrence of tandem duplications, which tend to be biased towards genes involved in responses to environmental stimuli<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hanada, K., Zou, C., Lehti-Shiu, M. D., Shinozaki, K. &amp; Shiu, S. H. Importance of lineage-specific expansion of plant tandem duplicates in the adaptive response to environmental stimuli. Plant Physiol. 148, 993â1003 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1713">22</a></sup>, was relatively low for <i>C. olitorius</i> (7.2% of total genes) and <i>C. capsularis</i> (5.9% of total genes) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 27</a>) compared with other plant genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Myburg, A. A. et al. The genome of Eucalyptus grandis. Nature 510, 356â362 (2014)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1727">23</a></sup>.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The genomics information of jute fibre biogenesis is merely available for the improvement of its yield and quality. RNA-seq data obtained from isolated fibre cells (elongated cells undergoing secondary cell wall (SCW) deposition) and seedlings of <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> were analysed to investigate the molecular events of jute fibre development (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 28 and 29</a>). We identified 6,077 upregulated and 6,809 downregulated genes for <i>C. olitorius</i> and 7,695 upregulated and 7,809 downregulated genes for <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 30 and 31</a>). Among them, 329 <i>C. olitorius</i> and 344 <i>C. capsularis</i> genes were identified based on the analysis of homologous genes reportedly involved in plant fibre formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gorshkova, T. et al. Plant fiber formation: state of the art, recent and expected progress, and open questions. Crit. Rev. Plant Sci. 31, 201â228 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1765">24</a></sup> which facilitated us to propose a model for bast fibre biogenesis in jute (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). It was found that 174 (53%) <i>C. olitorius</i> and 216 (63%) <i>C. capsularis</i> genes were expressed significantly within the fibre cells and seedlings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 32</a>). Genes encoding the WOX4, APL and HAT22 transcription factors and the TDIF signalling peptide, which are involved in vascular cambium initiation and proliferation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Ji, J. et al. WOX4 promotes procambial development. Plant Physiol. 152, 1346â1356 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1782">25</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Hirakawa, Y., Kondo, Y. &amp; Fukuda, H. Establishment and maintenance of vascular cell communities through local signaling. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 14, 1â7 (2010)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1785">28</a></sup>, were highly expressed in fibre cells, suggesting their importance in fibre differentiation. Moreover, several of the transcription factor genes involved in regulating SCW formation exhibited higher expression in the fibre cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). In particular, a substantial increase in expression was observed for the <i>MYB83</i> homologue of <i>Arabidopsis</i>, a master regulator capable of activating the biosynthesis of all major SCW components (cellulose, lignin and xylan)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="McCarthy, R. L., Zhong, R. &amp; Ye, Z. H. MYB83 is a direct target of SND1 and acts redundantly with MYB46 in the regulation of secondary cell wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Physiol. 50, 1950â1964 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1799">29</a></sup>. The homologue of <i>AtMYB46</i>, which is co-expressed and functionally redundant with <i>AtMYB83</i> in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="McCarthy, R. L., Zhong, R. &amp; Ye, Z. H. MYB83 is a direct target of SND1 and acts redundantly with MYB46 in the regulation of secondary cell wall biosynthesis in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell Physiol. 50, 1950â1964 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1812">29</a></sup>, showed little or no expression in jute fibres indicating that <i>MYB83</i> is primarily accountable for the SCW regulatory network of jute.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fibre development in jute."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Fibre development in jute.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants2016223/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.223/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants2016223_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.223/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants2016223_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="899"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Schematic representation of the fibre formation process in jute. Fibre formation-related genes are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 32</a>. <b>b</b>,<b>c</b>, Heat map showing relative expression of genes involved in the biosynthesis of lignin (<b>b</b>) and cellulose (<b>c</b>) between 4-day-old seedlings and fibre cells. A similar expression pattern was also observed between very young seedlings before bolting and fibre cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 12a1,a2</a>). The heat maps of normalized RNA-seq data was prepared from three biological replicates from fibre cells and whole seedlings of <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>. Gene expression was measured by quantified transcription levels (fragments per kilobase of exon model per million mapped reads, FPKM) derived from RNA-seq analysis. Heat scale, log2(FPKM). To calculate the log2(FPKM) values of individual genes, all of the original FPKM values were added by a pseudo-count of 1. CH4, cinnamate-4-hydroxylase; PAL, L-phenylalanine ammonia-lyase; HCT, hydroxycinnamoyl transferase; C3H, 4-coumarate 3-hydroxylase; F5H, ferulate-5-hydroxylase; CAD, cinnamyl alcohol dehydrogenase.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants2016223/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The relatively high lignin content (<span class="stix">â¼</span>15%) in jute fibre makes it coarser than other bast fibres such as flax and ramie (&lt;5% lignin)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Urquhart, A. R. &amp; Howitt, F. O. The Structure of Textile Fibres (Textile Institute, 1953)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1870">30</a></sup>. Among the lignin biosynthetic genes detected in the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genomes, there were expansions of the 4-coumarate:CoA ligase (4CL), cinnamoyl-CoA reductase (CCR), <i>trans</i>-caffeoyl-CoA 3-<i>O</i>-methyltransferase (CCoAOMT) and caffeic acid <i>O</i>-methyltransferase (COMT) gene families compared with flax (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 33</a>). The expression profiles of the lignification genes reveal that only a few homologues appeared to be preferentially expressed at high levels in the fibre cells for most of the gene families (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 12a1</a>), highlighting possible targets for engineering low-lignin jute fibres. Cellulose, synthesized by the cellulose synthase (CesA) complex, makes up the majority of the SCW in jute fibres (<span class="stix">â¼</span>60%). We identified 10 CesA and 32 cellulose synthase-like (Csl) genes, similar to several other plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 34</a>). SCW synthesis specific genes <i>CesA4</i> and <i>CesA7</i> were distinctly upregulated in fibre cells (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 12a2</a>) indicating their association with SCW cellulose deposition, whereas significant expression of <i>CesA1</i>, <i>CesA3</i> and <i>CesA6</i> in seedlings suggest their involvement in primary cell wall cellulose deposition (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig2">Fig. 2c</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 32</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 12a2</a>).</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>Senescence and cell death are the final phases of fibre biogenesis related to autophagy and proteolysis pathways. KEGG pathway enrichment analysis with fibre cell transcriptome data indicated an upregulation of autophagy and proteolysis pathways whereas most of the metabolic pathways were downregulated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 35 and 36</a>). In flax phloem and poplar xylem fibres, a gradual degradation of the nucleus and cytoplasm is likely to be mediated by autophagy while deposition of the SCW continues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Ageeva, M. V., Chernova, T. E. &amp; Gorshkova, T. A. Processes of protoplast senescence and death in flax fibers: an ultrastructural analysis. Russ. J. Dev. Biol. 43, 94â100 (2012)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1946">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Courtois-Moreau, C. L. et al. A unique program for cell death in xylem fibers of Populus stem. Plant J. 58, 260â274 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1949">32</a></sup>. In poplar xylem fibres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Courtois-Moreau, C. L. et al. A unique program for cell death in xylem fibers of Populus stem. Plant J. 58, 260â274 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1953">32</a></sup>, all copies of <i>ATG8</i> were upregulated in jute fibre cells and the expressions were the highest among the autophagy-related genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 32</a>) suggesting a similar cell death programme.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>RNA-seq results for fibre biogenesis pathway genes were validated with quantitative polymerase chain reaction with reverse transcription (RTâqPCR) on randomly selected genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 13</a>). For most of the genes, similar upregulation and downregulation patterns were observed.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>To explain the morphological and physiological differences between the species, we focused on comparing the transcripts and genes that are related to lignin deposition in SCW, fibre colour and response to abiotic stress. As <i>C. olitorius</i> fibre contains more lignin and less cellulose than that of <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 37</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Sur, D. &amp; Amin, M. N. in Jute Basics (ed. Sur, D. ) 35â55 (International Jute Study Group, 2010)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d47581e1981">33</a></sup>, expression patterns of lignin and cellulose biosynthesis genes between them were different (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig2">Fig. 2b,c</a>). Genes encoding most of the key enzymes for proanthocyanidin biosynthesis were highly expressed in the fibres of <i>C. olitorius</i> (golden colour) than in <i>C. capsularis</i> (whitish colour) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 14</a>), indicating their involvement for the differences in fibre pigmentation.</p></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p><i>C. capsularis</i> is comparatively tolerant to flood and drought but slightly more susceptible to diseases and pests than <i>C. olitorius</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Roy, A. et al. Evaluation of genetic diversity in jute (Corchorus species) using STMS, ISSR and RAPD markers. Plant Breed. 125, 292â297 (2006)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d47581e2005">34</a></sup>. Moreover, <i>C. capsularis</i> is somewhat tolerant to salt stress compared with <i>C. olitorius</i> and can survive up to 60âmM concentrations of NaCl in nutrient media (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 15</a>). We categorized gene ontology (GO) using the Blast2GO pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Conesa, A. &amp; GÃ¶tz, S. Blast2GO: a comprehensive suite for functional analysis in plant genomics. Intl. J. Plant Genomics 2008, 619832 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d47581e2019">35</a></sup> to identify gene copy number variation which is a major mechanism of phenotypic differentiation and evolutionary adaptation to the environment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. Transcription strength and halophytic lifestyle. Trends Plant Sci. 16, 1â3 (2011)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d47581e2023">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Hastings, P. J. et al. Mechanisms of change in gene copy number. Nat. Rev. Genet. 10, 551â564 (2009)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d47581e2026">37</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 38 and 39</a>). In the GO class âbiological processesâ, genes with GO terms associated with response to important environmental factors including salt and osmotic stress were over-represented in <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 40</a>). Besides, in the GO class âmolecular functionâ, we found that genes responding to abiotic stress were also significantly over-represented in <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 40</a>). Among them transmembrane transport, vacuolar transport, homeostatic process, ATPase activity, transmembrane transporter activity, signal transducer activity, oxidoreductase activity were significantly higher in <i>C. capsularis</i>, indicating its adaptability in different habitats and environmental pressure. For example, transmembrane transport proteins mediate ion fluxes, including sodium ATPase, vacuolar H<sup>+</sup>-ATPase, chloride channel protein, and ABC transporters play important roles in ionic and osmotic homeostasis under salt environments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Wang, X. et al. Proteomic analysis of the response to high-salinity stress in Physcomitrella patens. Planta 228, 167â177 (2008)." href="/articles/nplants2016223#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d47581e2057">38</a></sup>. To corroborate GO analysis results, correlated genes were identified (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 41</a>) and most of them are associated with abiotic stress tolerance.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparison of GO categories between C. olitorius and C. capsularis based on the response to abiotic stress."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparison of GO categories between <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> based on the response to abiotic stress.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants2016223/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.223/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants2016223_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnplants.2016.223/MediaObjects/41477_2017_Article_BFnplants2016223_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="807"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, GO biological processes. <b>b</b>, GO molecular functions. The list of gene numbers in each of the GO categories and their associated <i>P</i> values are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 40</a>. The GO categories are as defined in Blast2GO. Categories with significant differences calculated using a chi-squared test, as described in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Information</a>. The complete list of GO categories for <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genome are listed in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 38 and 39</a>. NS, non-significant. The remainder of the categories are significant at the 1% level. ORFs, open reading frames.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nplants2016223/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div><div class="c-article-section__content"><p>The comparative genome analysis opens up opportunities for the development of improved breeding strategies to meet the increasing demand of natural fibres in industry. The genome sequences provide a valuable resource to advance our understanding of bast fibre biogenesis in jute, thus serving as the foundation of genetic improvement for productivity and fibre quality.</p></div><section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec2">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The genomes of <i>C. olitorius</i> var. O-4 and <i>C. capsularis</i> var. CVL-1 were sequenced using the WGS approach on the 454 platform. A total of 13.04âGb of sequence data was generated for the <i>C. olitorius</i> genome, consisting of 5.65âGb of shotgun sequences, 2.56âGb of 3âkb paired-end sequences, 2.47âGb of 8âkb paired-end sequences and 2.36âGb of 20âkb paired-end sequences. For the <i>C. capsularis</i> genome, 13.69âGb of sequence data was generated, consisting of 7.87âGb of shotgun sequences, 2.04âGb of 3âkb paired-end sequences, 2.26âGb of 8âkb paired-end sequences and 1.51âGb of 20âkb paired-end sequences (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 1</a>).</p><p>We used the CABOG tool sffToCA to identify mated reads and remove duplicate mate pairs. The remaining read sequence data were converted to the fastq format, trimmed to a length of 65 bases and used in the assembly. The CABOG v7.0 pipelines were then run with default parameters using a kmer parameter of 22, which was selected after testing a range of kmer settings.</p><p>We used whole-genome optical mapping technology to improve and validate the assemblies (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 3</a>). A total of 360,906 and 260,615 single-molecule restriction maps longer than 250âkb each, with an average size of 356.37 and 356.99âkb, were generated using the Kpn<i>I</i> restriction enzyme for <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 4</a>). Super-scaffolding with optical map data was performed using Genome-Builder software (OpGen). Super-scaffolds and scaffolds were anchored to seven linkage groups using a combination of traditional markers and whole-genome mapping data using ALLMAPS software.</p><p>The accuracy and completeness of the assemblies were assessed by aligning isotigs that were generated from transcriptome sequencing onto the WGS scaffolds using BLAT (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 9</a>). We also checked the relative completeness of the assemblies by performing core gene annotation using the CEGMA v2.5 pipelines (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 10</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Gene annotation</h3><p>Repetitive elements were identified and masked by RepeatModeler v1.0.7 and RepeatMasker Open-3.0 with default parameters. Gene prediction was performed using a combination of homology, <i>de novo</i> and transcript-based approaches (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). The predicted genes were analysed for functional domains and homologies by using InterProScan and Basic Local Alignment Search Tool (BLAST) against the NCBI non-redundant database, TrEMBL and SwissProt with Protein BLAST (BLASTP) (eâ&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>) and Blast2GO v3.3 with default parameters.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Genome comparison and evolution</h3><p>Comparative analysis was performed to identify orthologous gene families among the genomes of <i>C. olitorius</i>, <i>C. capsularis</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, <i>Gossypium raimondii</i>, <i>Glycine max</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i>, <i>Linum usitatissimum</i>, <i>Medicago truncatula</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i>, <i>Solanum tuberosum</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>. All predicted protein sequences of these plants (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 20</a>) were searched against each other using BLASTP (eâ&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>) and clustered into orthologous groups using MCL-10-201 (inflation parameter, 1.5). Clusters containing single-copy orthologues were identified with exact one member per species. Phylogenetic relationships were determined from these single-copy orthologues using the maximum likelihood method.</p><p>Paralogous genes in <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> were detected by all-against-all protein sequence similarity searches using BLASTP. The synonymous substitution rate (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) was calculated for each gene pair. Paralogous genes were determined to be tandem duplicates if they were located within five genes from each other. Orthologous genes between <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> were identified using the reciprocal best hit method and the <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values were calculated for each pair. Intra- and intergenomic regions of synteny were identified and visualized by SyMAP v4.0.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Pathway reconstruction</h3><p>Metabolic and regulatory pathways were reconstructed with Pathway Studio software based on the Resnet-Plant 4.0 database. Predicted jute interologues and pathways were imported into a new Pathway Studio database for manual pathway reconstruction and genome analysis.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Fibre cell transcriptome sequencing and analysis</h3><p>The transcriptomes of isolated fibre cells and whole seedlings were sequenced with an Illumina HiSeq 2,500 at HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology, Huntsville, Alabama (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Tables 28 and 29</a>). Expression patterns were compared by aligning the RNA-seq reads against the <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> genome sequences and quantifying the transcript abundances using the Cufflinks v2.2.1 package, which was visualized by R libraries. KEGG Orthology Based Annotation System (KOBAS) was used to identify the pathways in the <i>C. olitorius</i> and the <i>C. capsularis</i> genome using the model organism <i>A. thaliana</i>. KEGG (Release 74.0) and Biocyc v19.0 pathways were utilized to run R package piano v1.8.0 for Gene set analysis (GSA). Pathways in the distinct direction were selected for subsequent analysis based on adjusted <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05. The differential gene expression from the <i>in silico</i> analysis were validated by RTâqPCR with randomly selected several fibre biosynthesis pathway genes. All primers used in this study are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM8">Supplementary Table 42</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Statistical analyses</h3><p>Two-tailed chi-squared tests were used to compare the distributions of GO subcategories between <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). For each GO subcategory, a 2âÃâ2 contingency table was constructed by recording the existence of the number of genes in a subcategory for each species and ranking the statistical significance of the differences.</p><p>Detailed methods and their associated references are in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nplants2016223#MOESM7">Supplementary Information</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Data availability</h3><p>The WGS projects have been deposited at NCBI GenBank under BioProject ID <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA215141">PRJNA215141</a> and accession no. <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=AWUE00000000">AWUE00000000</a> for <i>C. olitorius</i> and BioProject ID <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA215142">PRJNA215142</a> and accession no. <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=AWWV00000000">AWWV00000000</a> for <i>C. capsularis</i>. The genomic and transcriptomic raw data have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP049494">SRP049494</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRP053213">SRP053213</a> for <i>C. olitorius</i> and <i>C. capsularis</i>, respectively.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Additional information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Additional information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p><b>How to cite this article:</b> Islam, M. S. <i>et al</i>. Comparative genomics of two jute species and insight into fibre biogenesis. <i>Nat. Plants</i> <b>3</b>, 16223 (2017).</p></div></div></section>

M.M.A. and M.K.U. managed the project. M.S.I., M.S. Haque, Q.M.M.H., M.Z.H., R.H., N.A., U.H., R.I., and H.K. performed material preparation and multiplication, DNA and RNA extractions. S.H. and X.W. performed genome and transcriptome sequencing. M.S.I., E.M.E., M.M.I., M.S.R., M.M.R., M.S.A.K., S.M.T.K., M.M.A. and J.A.S. contributed to genome analysis including assembly, annotation, genome evolution and comparative genomics. M.S.I., R.A., B.A. and M.S. Hossain conducted fibre cell isolation for RNA extraction and conducted RTâqPCR of fibre biosynthesis genes. M.S.I., E.M.E., R.A., B.A., M.S. Hossain, S.M.T.K. and X.W. contributed to expression analysis of the fibre cell transcriptome. M.S.I., E.M.E., Q.M.M.H. and J.A.S. analysed the optical mapping data. B.A., A.H., M.M.K. and A.Y. conducted pathway and network analysis. M.S.I., M.S. Haque, M.M.I., A.H., M.Z.H., Q.M.M.H., J.A.S., T.H., M.S.A., M.M.M., S.M.E., A.M.M.H., N.M., M.S., S.S., N.S.S., S.J., S.R., A.C., A.I.A., G.M.N., K.S.U., T.R., S.M.E.H., A.R.S., S.M., S.A.M., M.K.I., M.Z.H.L., M.Z. and H.K. contributed to <i>de novo</i> genome assembly of <i>Corchorus olitorius</i>. M.S.I., J.A.S. and M.A. wrote and/or revised the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
